<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Credit default swap</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CDS-nodefault.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="628" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/CDS-nodefault.PNG/300px-CDS-nodefault.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/CDS-nodefault.PNG/450px-CDS-nodefault.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/CDS-nodefault.PNG/600px-CDS-nodefault.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CDS-nodefault.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
If the reference bond performs without default, the protection buyer pays quarterly payments to the seller until maturity</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CDS-default.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="628" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/CDS-default.PNG/300px-CDS-default.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/CDS-default.PNG/450px-CDS-default.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/CDS-default.PNG/600px-CDS-default.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CDS-default.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
If the reference bond defaults, the protection seller pays par value of the bond to the buyer, and the buyer transfers ownership of the bond to the seller</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>credit default swap</b> (<b>CDS</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Swap_(finance)" title="Swap (finance)">financial swap</a> agreement that the seller of the CDS will compensate the buyer (usually the creditor of the reference loan) in the event of a loan <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">default</a> (by the debtor) or other <a href="/wiki/Credit_event" title="Credit event">credit event</a>. This is to say that the seller of the CDS insures the buyer against some reference loan defaulting. The buyer of the CDS makes a series of payments (the CDS "fee" or "spread") to the seller and, in exchange, receives a payoff if the loan defaults. It was invented by <a href="/wiki/Blythe_Masters" title="Blythe Masters">Blythe Masters</a> from <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan_%26_Co." title="J.P. Morgan &amp; Co.">JP Morgan</a> in 1994.</p>
<p>In the event of default the buyer of the CDS receives compensation (usually the <a href="/wiki/Face_value" title="Face value">face value</a> of the loan), and the seller of the CDS takes possession of the defaulted loan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LBO_CDS_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-LBO_CDS-1">[1]</a></sup> However, anyone can purchase a CDS, even buyers who do not hold the loan instrument and who have no direct <a href="/wiki/Insurable_interest" title="Insurable interest">insurable interest</a> in the loan (these are called "naked" CDSs). If there are more CDS contracts outstanding than bonds in existence, a protocol exists to hold a <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap#Auctions" title="Credit default swap">credit event auction</a>; the payment received is usually substantially less than the face value of the loan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Credit default swaps have existed since 1994, and increased in use after 2003. By the end of 2007, the outstanding CDS amount was $62.2 trillion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISDA_Annual_Chart_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISDA_Annual_Chart-3">[3]</a></sup> falling to $26.3 trillion by mid-year 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-test_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-test-4">[4]</a></sup> and reportedly $25.5<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> trillion in early 2012. CDSs are not traded on an exchange and there is no required reporting of transactions to a government agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF254_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMF254-6">[6]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2010">2007-2010 financial crisis</a> the lack of transparency in this large market became a concern to regulators as it could pose a <a href="/wiki/Systemic_risk" title="Systemic risk">systemic risk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimkovicSecret_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SimkovicSecret-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sirri_Testimony_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sirri_Testimony-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partnoy_Article_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partnoy_Article-10">[10]</a></sup> In March 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Depository_Trust_%26_Clearing_Corporation" title="Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation">Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation</a> (see <a href="#Sources_of_market_data">Sources of Market Data</a>) announced it would give regulators greater access to its credit default swaps database.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>CDS data can be used by <a href="/wiki/Financial_risk_management" title="Financial risk management">financial professionals</a>, regulators, and the media to monitor how the market views <a href="/wiki/Credit_risk" title="Credit risk">credit risk</a> of any entity on which a CDS is available, which can be compared to that provided by the <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agency" title="Credit rating agency">Credit Rating Agencies</a>. U.S. Courts may soon be following suit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LBO_CDS_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-LBO_CDS-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most CDSs are documented using standard forms drafted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association" title="International Swaps and Derivatives Association">International Swaps and Derivatives Association (ISDA)</a>, although there are many variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup> In addition to the basic, single-name swaps, there are <a href="/wiki/Basket_(finance)" title="Basket (finance)">basket</a> default swaps (BDSs), index CDSs, funded CDSs (also called <a href="/wiki/Credit-linked_note" title="Credit-linked note">credit-linked notes</a>), as well as loan-only credit default swaps (LCDS). In addition to corporations and governments, the reference entity can include a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_purpose_vehicle" title="Special purpose vehicle">special purpose vehicle</a> issuing <a href="/wiki/Asset-backed_security" title="Asset-backed security">asset-backed securities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some claim that derivatives such as CDS are potentially dangerous in that they combine priority in bankruptcy with a lack of transparency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimkovicSecret_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-SimkovicSecret-8">[8]</a></sup> A CDS can be unsecured (without collateral) and be at higher risk for a default.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="540" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg/180px-Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg/270px-Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg/360px-Cds_paymentstream_protection_noloss.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Buyer purchased a CDS at time t<sub>0</sub> and makes regular premium payments at times t<sub>1</sub>, t<sub>2</sub>, t<sub>3</sub>, and t<sub>4</sub>. If the associated credit instrument suffers no credit event, then the buyer continues paying premiums at t<sub>5</sub>, t<sub>6</sub> and so on until the end of the contract at time t<sub>n</sub>.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="540" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg/180px-Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg/270px-Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg/360px-Cds_paymentstream_protection_loss_event.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">However, if the associated credit instrument suffered a credit event at t<sub>5</sub>, then the seller pays the buyer for the loss, and the buyer would cease paying premiums to the seller.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A CDS is linked to a "reference entity" or "reference obligor", usually a corporation or government. The reference entity is not a party to the contract. The buyer makes regular premium payments to the seller, the premium amounts constituting the "spread" charged by the seller to insure against a credit event. If the reference entity defaults, the protection seller pays the buyer the <a href="/wiki/Par_value" title="Par value">par value</a> of the bond in exchange for physical delivery of the bond, although settlement may also be by cash or auction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association.2C_Inc._ISDA_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association.2C_Inc._ISDA-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>A default is often referred to as a "credit event" and includes such events as failure to pay, restructuring and bankruptcy, or even a drop in the borrower's <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">credit rating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup> CDS contracts on sovereign obligations also usually include as credit events repudiation, moratorium and acceleration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF254_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-IMF254-6">[6]</a></sup> Most CDSs are in the $10–$20 million range<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB_Primer_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FRB_Primer-15">[15]</a></sup> with maturities between one and 10 years. Five years is the most typical maturity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>An investor or speculator may “buy protection” to hedge the risk of default on a bond or other debt instrument, regardless of whether such investor or speculator holds an interest in or bears any risk of loss relating to such bond or debt instrument. In this way, a CDS is similar to <a href="/wiki/Credit_derivative" title="Credit derivative">credit insurance</a>, although CDS are not subject to regulations governing traditional insurance. Also, investors can buy and sell protection without owning debt of the reference entity. These "naked credit default swaps" allow traders to speculate on the creditworthiness of reference entities. CDSs can be used to create synthetic long and short positions in the reference entity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sirri_Testimony_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sirri_Testimony-9">[9]</a></sup> Naked CDS constitute most of the market in CDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kopecki1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-kopecki1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup> In addition, CDSs can also be used in capital structure <a href="/wiki/Arbitrage" title="Arbitrage">arbitrage</a>.</p>
<p>A "credit default swap" (CDS) is a <a href="/wiki/Credit_derivative" title="Credit derivative">credit derivative</a> contract between two <a href="/wiki/Counterparty" title="Counterparty">counterparties</a>. The buyer makes periodic payments to the seller, and in return receives a payoff if an underlying financial instrument <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">defaults</a> or experiences a similar <a href="/wiki/Credit_event" title="Credit event">credit event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association.2C_Inc._ISDA_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association.2C_Inc._ISDA-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The CDS may refer to a specified loan or bond obligation of a “reference entity”, usually a corporation or government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB_Primer_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FRB_Primer-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>As an example, imagine that an investor buys a CDS from AAA-Bank, where the reference entity is Risky Corp. The investor—the buyer of protection—will make regular payments to AAA-Bank—the seller of protection. <i>If</i> Risky Corp defaults on its debt, the investor receives a one-time payment from AAA-Bank, and the CDS contract is terminated.</p>
<p>If the investor actually owns Risky Corp's debt (i.e., is owed money by Risky Corp), a CDS can act as a <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedge</a>. But investors can also buy CDS contracts referencing Risky Corp debt without actually owning any Risky Corp debt. This may be done for speculative purposes, to bet against the solvency of Risky Corp in a gamble to make money, or to hedge investments in other companies whose fortunes are expected to be similar to those of Risky Corp (see <a href="#Uses">Uses</a>).</p>
<p>If the reference entity (i.e., Risky Corp) defaults, one of two kinds of <a href="#Settlement">settlement</a> can occur:</p>
<ul>
<li>the investor delivers a defaulted asset to Bank for payment of the <a href="/wiki/Par_value" title="Par value">par value</a>, which is known as <i>physical settlement</i>;</li>
<li>AAA-Bank pays the investor the difference between the par value and the market price of a specified debt obligation (even if Risky Corp defaults there is usually some <i>recovery</i>, i.e., not all the investor's money is lost), which is known as <i>cash settlement</i>.</li>
</ul>
<p>The "spread" of a CDS is the annual amount the protection buyer must pay the protection seller over the length of the contract, expressed as a percentage of the <a href="/wiki/Notional_amount" title="Notional amount">notional amount</a>. For example, if the CDS spread of Risky Corp is 50 <a href="/wiki/Basis_point" title="Basis point">basis points</a>, or 0.5% (1 basis point = 0.01%), then an investor buying $10 million worth of protection from AAA-Bank must pay the bank $50,000. Payments are usually made on a quarterly basis, in <a href="/wiki/Arrears" title="Arrears">arrears</a>. These payments continue until either the CDS contract expires or Risky Corp defaults.</p>
<p>All things being equal, at any given time, if the maturity of two credit default swaps is the same, then the CDS associated with a company with a <i>higher</i> CDS spread is considered <i>more likely</i> to default by the market, since a higher fee is being charged to protect against this happening. However, factors such as liquidity and estimated loss given default can affect the comparison. Credit spread rates and credit ratings of the underlying or reference obligations are considered among money managers to be the best indicators of the likelihood of sellers of CDSs having to perform under these contracts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_from_insurance">Differences from insurance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Differences from insurance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CDS contracts have obvious similarities with insurance, because the buyer pays a premium and, in return, receives a sum of money if an adverse event occurs.</p>
<p>However, there are also many differences, the most important being that an insurance contract provides an indemnity against the losses <i>actually suffered</i> by the policy holder on an asset in which it holds an <a href="/wiki/Insurable_interest" title="Insurable interest">insurable interest</a>. By contrast a CDS provides an equal payout to all holders, calculated using an agreed, market-wide method. The holder does not need to own the underlying <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">security</a> and does not even have to suffer a loss from the default event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The CDS can therefore be used to speculate on debt objects.</p>
<p>The other differences include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The seller might in principle not be a regulated entity (though in practice most are banks);</li>
<li>The seller is not required to maintain reserves to cover the protection sold (this was a principal cause of AIG's financial distress in 2008; it had insufficient reserves to meet the "run" of expected payouts caused by the collapse of the housing bubble);</li>
<li>Insurance requires the buyer to disclose all known risks, while CDSs do not (the CDS seller can in many cases still determine potential risk, as the debt instrument being "insured" is a market commodity available for inspection, but in the case of certain instruments like <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">CDOs</a> made up of "slices" of debt packages, it can be difficult to tell exactly what is being insured);</li>
<li>Insurers manage risk primarily by setting <a href="/wiki/Loss_reserving" title="Loss reserving">loss reserves</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_large_numbers" title="Law of large numbers">Law of large numbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actuarial_science" title="Actuarial science">actuarial analysis</a>. Dealers in CDSs manage risk primarily by means of hedging with other CDS deals and in the underlying bond markets;</li>
<li>CDS contracts are generally subject to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark-to-market" title="Mark-to-market">mark-to-market</a> accounting, introducing <a href="/wiki/Income_statement" title="Income statement">income statement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balance_sheet" title="Balance sheet">balance sheet</a> <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(finance)" title="Volatility (finance)">volatility</a> while insurance contracts are not;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hedge_accounting" title="Hedge accounting">Hedge accounting</a> may not be available under US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generally_Accepted_Accounting_Principles" title="Generally Accepted Accounting Principles">Generally Accepted Accounting Principles</a> (GAAP) unless the requirements of <a class="external text" href="http://www.fasb.org/st/summary/stsum133.shtml" rel="nofollow">FAS 133</a> are met. In practice this rarely happens.</li>
<li>to cancel the insurance contract the buyer can typically stop paying premiums, while for CDS the contract needs to be unwound.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk">Risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When entering into a CDS, both the buyer and seller of credit protection take on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterparty_risk" title="Counterparty risk">counterparty risk</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The buyer takes the risk that the seller may default. If AAA-Bank and Risky Corp. default simultaneously ("<a href="/wiki/Double_default" title="Double default">double default</a>"), the buyer loses its protection against default by the reference entity. If AAA-Bank defaults but Risky Corp. does not, the buyer might need to replace the defaulted CDS at a higher cost.</li>
<li>The seller takes the risk that the buyer may default on the contract, depriving the seller of the expected revenue stream. More important, a seller normally limits its risk by buying offsetting protection from another party — that is, it hedges its exposure. If the original buyer drops out, the seller squares its position by either unwinding the hedge transaction or by selling a new CDS to a third party. Depending on market conditions, that may be at a lower price than the original CDS and may therefore involve a loss to the seller.</li>
</ul>
<p>In the future, in the event that regulatory reforms require that CDS be traded and settled via a central exchange/<a href="/wiki/Clearing_house_(finance)" title="Clearing house (finance)">clearing house</a>, such as ICE TCC, there will no longer be 'counterparty risk', as the risk of the counterparty will be held with the central exchange/clearing house.</p>
<p>As is true with other forms of over-the-counter derivative, CDS might involve <a href="/wiki/Liquidity_risk" title="Liquidity risk">liquidity risk</a>. If one or both parties to a CDS contract must post collateral (which is common), there can be margin calls requiring the posting of additional <a href="/wiki/Collateral_(finance)" title="Collateral (finance)">collateral</a>. The required collateral is agreed on by the parties when the CDS is first issued. This <a href="/wiki/Margin_(finance)" title="Margin (finance)">margin</a> amount may vary over the life of the CDS contract, if the market price of the CDS contract changes, or the <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">credit rating</a> of one of the parties changes. Many CDS contracts even require payment of an upfront fee (composed of "reset to par" and an "initial coupon.").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kramer_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-kramer-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another kind of risk for the seller of credit default swaps is jump risk or jump-to-default risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup> A seller of a CDS could be collecting monthly premiums with little expectation that the reference entity may default. A default creates a sudden obligation on the protection sellers to pay millions, if not billions, of dollars to protection buyers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gensler_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gensler-25">[25]</a></sup> This risk is not present in other over-the-counter derivatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gensler_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gensler-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_market_data">Sources of market data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sources of market data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Data about the credit default swaps market is available from three main sources. Data on an annual and semiannual basis is available from the International Swaps and Derivatives Association (ISDA) since 2001<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and from the Bank for International Settlements (BIS) since 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation (DTCC), through its global repository Trade Information Warehouse (TIW), provides weekly data but publicly available information goes back only one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The numbers provided by each source do not always match because each provider uses different sampling methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup> Daily, intraday and real time data is available from S&amp;P Capital IQ through their acquisition of Credit Market Analysis in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to DTCC, the Trade Information Warehouse maintains the only "global electronic database for virtually all CDS contracts outstanding in the marketplace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Office of the Comptroller of the Currency publishes quarterly credit derivative data about insured U.S commercial banks and trust companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Credit default swaps can be used by investors for <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arbitrage" title="Arbitrage">arbitrage</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speculation">Speculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Speculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Credit default swaps allow investors to speculate on changes in CDS spreads of single names or of market indices such as the North American CDX index or the European iTraxx index. An investor might believe that an entity's CDS spreads are too high or too low, relative to the entity's bond yields, and attempt to profit from that view by entering into a trade, known as a <a href="/wiki/Basis_trading" title="Basis trading">basis trade</a>, that combines a CDS with a cash bond and an <a href="/wiki/Interest_rate_swap" title="Interest rate swap">interest rate swap</a>.</p>
<p>Finally, an investor might speculate on an entity's credit quality, since generally CDS spreads increase as credit-worthiness declines, and decline as credit-worthiness increases. The investor might therefore buy CDS protection on a company to speculate that it is about to default. Alternatively, the investor might sell protection if it thinks that the company's creditworthiness might improve. The investor selling the CDS is viewed as being “<a href="/wiki/Long_(finance)" title="Long (finance)">long</a>” on the CDS and the credit, as if the investor owned the bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sirri_Testimony_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sirri_Testimony-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup> In contrast, the investor who bought protection is “<a href="/wiki/Short_(finance)" title="Short (finance)">short</a>” on the CDS and the underlying credit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sirri_Testimony_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sirri_Testimony-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Credit default swaps opened up important new avenues to speculators. Investors could go long on a bond without any upfront cost of buying a bond; all the investor need do was promise to pay in the <a href="/wiki/Event_of_default" title="Event of default">event of default</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDO-Lucas_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDO-Lucas-32">[32]</a></sup> Shorting a bond faced difficult practical problems, such that shorting was often not feasible; CDS made shorting credit possible and popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDO-Lucas_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDO-Lucas-32">[32]</a></sup> Because the speculator in either case does not own the bond, its position is said to be a synthetic long or short position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sirri_Testimony_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sirri_Testimony-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, a <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a> believes that Risky Corp will soon default on its debt. Therefore, it buys $10 million worth of CDS protection for two years from AAA-Bank, with Risky Corp as the reference entity, at a spread of 500 basis points (=5%) per annum.</p>
<ul>
<li>If Risky Corp does indeed default after, say, one year, then the hedge fund will have paid $500,000 to AAA-Bank, but then receives $10 million (assuming zero recovery rate, and that AAA-Bank has the liquidity to cover the loss), thereby making a profit. AAA-Bank, and its investors, will incur a $9.5 million loss minus recovery unless the bank has somehow offset the position before the default.</li>
<li>However, if Risky Corp does not default, then the CDS contract runs for two years, and the hedge fund ends up paying $1 million, without any return, thereby making a loss. AAA-Bank, by selling protection, has made $1 million without any upfront investment.</li>
</ul>
<p>Note that there is a third possibility in the above scenario; the hedge fund could decide to liquidate its position after a certain period of time in an attempt to realise its gains or losses. For example:</p>
<ul>
<li>After 1 year, the market now considers Risky Corp <i>more likely</i> to default, so its CDS spread has <i>widened</i> from 500 to 1500 basis points. The hedge fund may choose to <i>sell</i> $10 million worth of protection for 1 year to AAA-Bank at this higher rate. Therefore, over the two years the hedge fund pays the bank 2 * 5% * $10 million = $1 million, but receives 1 * 15% * $10 million = $1.5 million, giving a total profit of $500,000.</li>
<li>In another scenario, after one year the market now considers Risky much <i>less likely</i> to default, so its CDS spread has <i>tightened</i> from 500 to 250 basis points. Again, the hedge fund may choose to <i>sell</i> $10 million worth of protection for 1 year to AAA-Bank at this lower spread. Therefore, over the two years the hedge fund pays the bank 2 * 5% * $10 million = $1 million, but receives 1 * 2.5% * $10 million = $250,000, giving a total loss of $750,000. This loss is smaller than the $1 million loss that would have occurred if the second transaction had not been entered into.</li>
</ul>
<p>Transactions such as these do not even have to be entered into over the long-term. If Risky Corp's CDS spread had widened by just a couple of basis points over the course of one day, the hedge fund could have entered into an offsetting contract immediately and made a small profit over the life of the two CDS contracts.</p>
<p>Credit default swaps are also used to structure synthetic <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDOs). Instead of owning bonds or loans, a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDO</a> gets credit exposure to a portfolio of fixed income assets without owning those assets through the use of CDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partnoy_Article_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Partnoy_Article-10">[10]</a></sup> CDOs are viewed as complex and opaque financial instruments. An example of a synthetic CDO is Abacus 2007-AC1, which is the subject of the civil suit for fraud brought by the SEC against <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> in April 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Abacus is a synthetic CDO consisting of credit default swaps referencing a variety of <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Naked_credit_default_swaps">Naked credit default swaps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Naked credit default swaps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><span id="naked_credit_default_swap"></span>In the examples above, the hedge fund did not own any debt of Risky Corp. A CDS in which the buyer does not own the underlying debt is referred to as a <i>naked credit default swap</i>, estimated to be up to 80% of the credit default swap market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kopecki1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-kopecki1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup> There is currently a debate in the United States and Europe about whether speculative uses of credit default swaps should be banned. Legislation is under consideration by Congress as part of financial reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics assert that naked CDSs should be banned, comparing them to buying fire insurance on your neighbor’s house, which creates a huge incentive for arson. Analogizing to the concept of <a href="/wiki/Insurable_interest" title="Insurable interest">insurable interest</a>, critics say you should not be able to buy a CDS—insurance against default—when you do not own the bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings_Paper_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings_Paper-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buiter_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buiter-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Short selling is also viewed as gambling and the CDS market as a casino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leopold2009_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leopold2009-37">[37]</a></sup> Another concern is the size of the CDS market. Because naked credit default swaps are synthetic, there is no limit to how many can be sold. The gross amount of CDSs far exceeds all “real” corporate bonds and loans outstanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF254_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-IMF254-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buiter_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buiter-35">[35]</a></sup> As a result, the risk of default is magnified leading to concerns about systemic risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buiter_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Buiter-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Financier <a href="/wiki/George_Soros" title="George Soros">George Soros</a> called for an outright ban on naked credit default swaps, viewing them as “toxic” and allowing speculators to bet against and “bear raid” companies or countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soros_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soros-38">[38]</a></sup> His concerns were echoed by several European politicians who, during the <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">Greek Financial Crisis</a>, accused naked CDS buyers of making the crisis worse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crackdown_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crackdown-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greek_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greek-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite these concerns, Secretary of Treasury Geithner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crackdown_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crackdown-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a> Chairman Gensler<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> are not in favor of an outright ban on naked credit default swaps. They prefer greater transparency and better capitalization requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gensler_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gensler-25">[25]</a></sup> These officials think that naked CDSs have a place in the market.</p>
<p>Proponents of naked credit default swaps say that short selling in various forms, whether credit default swaps, options or futures, has the beneficial effect of increasing liquidity in the marketplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings_Paper_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings_Paper-34">[34]</a></sup> That benefits hedging activities. Without speculators buying and selling naked CDSs, banks wanting to hedge might not find a ready seller of protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings_Paper_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings_Paper-34">[34]</a></sup> Speculators also create a more competitive marketplace, keeping prices down for hedgers. A robust market in credit default swaps can also serve as a barometer to regulators and investors about the credit health of a company or country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings_Paper_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings_Paper-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Short_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Short-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite assertions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that speculators are making the Greek crisis worse, Germany's market regulator BaFin found no proof supporting the claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greek_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greek-40">[40]</a></sup> Some suggest<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> that without credit default swaps, Greece’s borrowing costs would be higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greek_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greek-40">[40]</a></sup> As of November 2011, the Greek bonds have a bond yield of 28%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>A bill in the U.S. Congress proposed giving a public authority the power to limit the use of CDSs other than for hedging purposes, but the bill did not become law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hedging">Hedging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hedging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Credit default swaps are often used to manage the risk of default that arises from holding debt. A bank, for example, may hedge its risk that a borrower may default on a loan by entering into a CDS contract as the buyer of protection. If the loan goes into default, the proceeds from the CDS contract cancel out the losses on the underlying debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB_Primer_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FRB_Primer-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are other ways to eliminate or reduce the risk of default. The bank could sell (that is, assign) the loan outright or bring in other banks as <a href="/wiki/Participation_loan" title="Participation loan">participants</a>. However, these options may not meet the bank’s needs. Consent of the corporate borrower is often required. The bank may not want to incur the time and cost to find loan participants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partnoy_Article_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Partnoy_Article-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>If both the borrower and lender are well-known and the market (or even worse, the news media) learns that the bank is selling the loan, then the sale may be viewed as signaling a lack of trust in the borrower, which could severely damage the banker-client relationship. In addition, the bank simply may not want to sell or share the potential profits from the loan. By buying a credit default swap, the bank can lay off default risk while still keeping the loan in its portfolio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partnoy_Article_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Partnoy_Article-10">[10]</a></sup> The downside to this hedge is that without default risk, a bank may have no motivation to actively monitor the loan and the counterparty has no relationship to the borrower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partnoy_Article_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Partnoy_Article-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another kind of hedge is against concentration risk. A bank’s risk management team may advise that the bank is overly concentrated with a particular borrower or industry. The bank can lay off some of this risk by buying a CDS. Because the borrower—the reference entity—is not a party to a credit default swap, entering into a CDS allows the bank to achieve its diversity objectives without impacting its loan portfolio or customer relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup> Similarly, a bank selling a CDS can diversify its portfolio by gaining exposure to an industry in which the selling bank has no customer base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FRB_Primer_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-FRB_Primer-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCC_Bulletin_96-43_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCC_Bulletin_96-43-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>A bank buying protection can also use a CDS to free regulatory capital. By offloading a particular credit risk, a bank is not required to hold as much capital in reserve against the risk of default (traditionally 8% of the total loan under <a href="/wiki/Basel_I" title="Basel I">Basel I</a>). This frees resources the bank can use to make other loans to the same key customer or to other borrowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arriva_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arriva-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hedging risk is not limited to banks as lenders. Holders of corporate bonds, such as banks, pension funds or insurance companies, may buy a CDS as a hedge for similar reasons. Pension fund example: A pension fund owns five-year bonds issued by Risky Corp with par value of $10 million. To manage the risk of losing money if Risky Corp defaults on its debt, the pension fund buys a CDS from Derivative Bank in a <a href="/wiki/Notional_amount" title="Notional amount">notional amount</a> of $10 million. The CDS trades at 200 <a href="/wiki/Basis_point" title="Basis point">basis points</a> (200 basis points = 2.00 percent). In return for this credit protection, the pension fund pays 2% of $10 million ($200,000) per annum in quarterly installments of $50,000 to Derivative Bank.</p>
<ul>
<li>If Risky Corporation does not default on its bond payments, the pension fund makes quarterly payments to Derivative Bank for 5 years and receives its $10 million back after five years from Risky Corp. Though the protection payments totaling $1 million reduce investment returns for the pension fund, its risk of loss due to Risky Corp defaulting on the bond is eliminated.</li>
<li>If Risky Corporation defaults on its debt three years into the CDS contract, the pension fund would stop paying the quarterly premium, and Derivative Bank would ensure that the pension fund is refunded for its loss of $10 million minus recovery (either by physical or cash settlement — see Settlement <a href="#Settlement">below</a>). The pension fund still loses the $600,000 it has paid over three years, but without the CDS contract it would have lost the entire $10 million minus recovery.</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to financial institutions, large suppliers can use a credit default swap on a public bond issue or a basket of similar risks as a proxy for its own credit risk exposure on receivables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings_Paper_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings_Paper-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arriva_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arriva-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although credit default swaps have been highly criticized for their role in the recent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2010">financial crisis</a>, most observers conclude that using credit default swaps as a hedging device has a useful purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings_Paper_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings_Paper-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arbitrage">Arbitrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arbitrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Capital Structure Arbitrage</i> is an example of an <a href="/wiki/Arbitrage" title="Arbitrage">arbitrage</a> strategy that uses CDS transactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This technique relies on the fact that a company's stock price and its CDS spread should exhibit negative correlation; i.e., if the outlook for a company improves then its share price should go up and its CDS spread should tighten, since it is less likely to default on its debt. However, if its outlook worsens then its CDS spread should widen and its stock price should fall.</p>
<p>Techniques reliant on this are known as <a href="/wiki/Capital_structure" title="Capital structure">capital structure</a> arbitrage because they exploit market inefficiencies between different parts of the same company's capital structure; i.e., mis-pricings between a company's debt and equity. An arbitrageur attempts to exploit the <i>spread</i> between a company's CDS and its equity in certain situations.</p>
<p>For example, if a company has announced some bad news and its share price has dropped by 25%, but its CDS spread has remained unchanged, then an investor might expect the CDS spread to increase relative to the share price. Therefore, a basic strategy would be to go long on the CDS spread (by buying CDS protection) while simultaneously hedging oneself by buying the underlying stock. This technique would benefit in the event of the CDS spread widening relative to the equity price, but would lose money if the company's CDS spread tightened relative to its equity.</p>
<p>An interesting situation in which the inverse correlation between a company's stock price and CDS spread breaks down is during a <a href="/wiki/Leveraged_buyout" title="Leveraged buyout">Leveraged buyout</a> (LBO). Frequently this leads to the company's CDS spread widening due to the extra debt that will soon be put on the company's books, but also an <i>increase</i> in its share price, since buyers of a company usually end up paying a premium.</p>
<p>Another common arbitrage strategy aims to exploit the fact that the swap-adjusted spread of a CDS should trade closely with that of the underlying cash bond issued by the reference entity. Misalignments in spreads may occur due to technical reasons such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Specific settlement differences</li>
<li>Shortages in a particular underlying instrument</li>
<li>The cost of funding a position</li>
<li>Existence of buyers constrained from buying exotic derivatives.</li>
</ul>
<p>The difference between CDS spreads and <a href="/wiki/Asset_swap" title="Asset swap">asset swap</a> spreads is called the <i>basis</i> and should theoretically be close to zero. Basis trades can aim to exploit any differences to make risk-free profit.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conception">Conception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Conception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Forms of credit default swaps had been in existence from at least the early 1990s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithson_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithson-49">[49]</a></sup> with early trades carried out by <a href="/wiki/Bankers_Trust" title="Bankers Trust">Bankers Trust</a> in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoolsGold_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FoolsGold-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan_%26_Co." title="J.P. Morgan &amp; Co.">J.P. Morgan &amp; Co.</a> is widely credited with creating the modern credit default swap in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philips_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philips-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tett.2C_Gillian_2006_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tett.2C_Gillian_2006-53">[53]</a></sup> In that instance, J.P. Morgan had extended a $4.8 billion credit line to <a href="/wiki/Exxon" title="Exxon">Exxon</a>, which faced the threat of $5 billion in <a href="/wiki/Punitive_damages" title="Punitive damages">punitive damages</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Valdez_oil_spill" title="Exxon Valdez oil spill">Exxon Valdez oil spill</a>. A team of J.P. Morgan bankers led by <a href="/wiki/Blythe_Masters" title="Blythe Masters">Blythe Masters</a> then sold the credit risk from the credit line to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Bank_of_Reconstruction_and_Development" title="European Bank of Reconstruction and Development">European Bank of Reconstruction and Development</a> in order to cut the reserves that J.P. Morgan was required to hold against Exxon's default, thus improving its own balance sheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1997, JPMorgan developed a proprietary product called BISTRO (Broad Index Securitized Trust Offering) that used CDS to clean up a bank’s balance sheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philips_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Philips-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tett.2C_Gillian_2006_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tett.2C_Gillian_2006-53">[53]</a></sup> The advantage of BISTRO was that it used securitization to split up the credit risk into little pieces that smaller investors found more digestible, since most investors lacked EBRD's capability to accept $4.8 billion in credit risk all at once. BISTRO was the first example of what later became known as synthetic <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDOs).</p>
<p>Mindful of the concentration of default risk as one of the causes of the <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_crisis" title="Savings and loan crisis">S&amp;L crisis</a>, regulators initially found CDS's ability to disperse default risk attractive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoolsGold_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FoolsGold-50">[50]</a></sup> In 2000, credit default swaps became largely exempt from regulation by both the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) and the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a> (CFTC). The <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Modernization_Act_of_2000" title="Commodity Futures Modernization Act of 2000">Commodity Futures Modernization Act of 2000</a>, which was also responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Enron_loophole" title="Enron loophole">Enron loophole</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF254_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-IMF254-6">[6]</a></sup> specifically stated that CDSs are neither futures nor securities and so are outside the remit of the SEC and CFTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoolsGold_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-FoolsGold-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_growth">Market growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Market growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At first, banks were the dominant players in the market, as CDS were primarily used to hedge risk in connection with its lending activities. Banks also saw an opportunity to free up regulatory capital. By March 1998, the global market for CDS was estimated at about $300 billion, with JP Morgan alone accounting for about $50 billion of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoolsGold_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-FoolsGold-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>The high market share enjoyed by the banks was soon eroded as more and more asset managers and hedge funds saw trading opportunities in credit default swaps. By 2002, investors as speculators, rather than banks as hedgers, dominated the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arriva_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Arriva-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithson_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-smithson-49">[49]</a></sup> National banks in the USA used credit default swaps as early as 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCC_Bulletin_96-43_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-OCC_Bulletin_96-43-45">[45]</a></sup> In that year, the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency measured the size of the market as tens of billions of dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Six years later, by year-end 2002, the outstanding amount was over $2 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISDA_Annual_Chart_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISDA_Annual_Chart-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although speculators fueled the exponential growth, other factors also played a part. An extended market could not emerge until 1999, when ISDA standardized the documentation for credit default swaps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranciere_IMF_Paper_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ranciere_IMF_Paper-58">[58]</a></sup> Also, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1997_Asian_Financial_Crisis" title="1997 Asian Financial Crisis">1997 Asian Financial Crisis</a> spurred a market for CDS in emerging market sovereign debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranciere_IMF_Paper_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ranciere_IMF_Paper-58">[58]</a></sup> In addition, in 2004, index trading began on a large scale and grew rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The market size for Credit Default Swaps more than doubled in size each year from $3.7 trillion in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISDA_Annual_Chart_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-ISDA_Annual_Chart-3">[3]</a></sup> By the end of 2007, the CDS market had a notional value of $62.2 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISDA_Annual_Chart_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-ISDA_Annual_Chart-3">[3]</a></sup> But notional amount fell during 2008 as a result of dealer "portfolio compression" efforts (replacing offsetting redundant contracts), and by the end of 2008 notional amount outstanding had fallen 38 percent to $38.6 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Explosive growth was not without operational headaches. On September 15, 2005, the New York Fed summoned 14 banks to its offices. Billions of dollars of CDS were traded daily but the record keeping was more than two weeks behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> This created severe risk management issues, as counterparties were in legal and financial limbo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mengle_Overview_13-9"><a href="#cite_note-Mengle_Overview-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> U.K. authorities expressed the same concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_as_of_2008">Market as of 2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Market as of 2008">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="530" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png/220px-Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png/330px-Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png/440px-Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Credit_default_swaps_by_quality_size_coloured_sp_percent_years.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Composition of the United States 15.5 trillion US dollar CDS market at the end of 2008 Q2. Green tints show Prime asset CDSs, reddish tints show sub-prime asset CDSs. Numbers followed by "Y" indicate years until maturity.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1646" data-file-width="1852" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png/220px-Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png/330px-Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png/440px-Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Credit_default_swaps_vs_total_nominals_plus_debt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Proportion of CDSs nominals (lower left) held by United States banks compared to all derivatives, in 2008Q2. The black disc represents the 2008 public debt.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since default is a relatively rare occurrence (historically around 0.2% of investment grade companies default in any one year),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> in most CDS contracts the only payments are the premium payments from buyer to seller. Thus, although the above figures for outstanding notionals are very large, in the absence of default the net cash flows are only a small fraction of this total: for a 100 bp = 1% spread, the annual cash flows are only 1% of the notional amount.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulatory_concerns_over_CDS">Regulatory concerns over CDS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Regulatory concerns over CDS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The market for Credit Default Swaps attracted considerable concern from regulators after a number of large scale incidents in 2008, starting with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collapse_of_Bear_Stearns" title="Collapse of Bear Stearns">collapse of Bear Stearns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colin_Barr_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_Barr-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the days and weeks leading up to Bear's collapse, the bank's CDS spread widened dramatically, indicating a surge of buyers taking out protection on the bank. It has been suggested that this widening was responsible for the perception that Bear Stearns was vulnerable, and therefore restricted its access to wholesale capital, which eventually led to its forced sale to <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JP Morgan</a> in March. An alternative view is that this surge in CDS protection buyers was a <i>symptom</i> rather than a <i>cause</i> of Bear's collapse; i.e., investors saw that Bear was in trouble, and sought to hedge any naked exposure to the bank, or speculate on its collapse.</p>
<p>In September, the <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_of_Lehman_Brothers" title="Bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers">bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers</a> caused a total close to $400 billion to become payable to the buyers of CDS protection referenced against the insolvent bank.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However the net amount that changed hands was around $7.2 billion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_given_citation_does_not_support_either_of_the_two_purported_facts_stated_in_previous_two_sentences.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The given citation does not support either of the two purported facts stated in previous two sentences. (page does not exist)">The given citation does not support either of the two purported facts stated in previous two sentences.</a>). This difference is due to the process of 'netting'. Market participants co-operated so that CDS sellers were allowed to deduct from their payouts the inbound funds due to them from their hedging positions. Dealers generally attempt to remain risk-neutral, so that their losses and gains after big events offset each other.</p>
<p>Also in September American International Group (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIG" title="AIG">AIG</a>) required <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> a $85 billion federal loan because it had been excessively selling CDS protection without hedging against the possibility that the reference entities might decline in value, which exposed the insurance giant to potential losses over $100 billion. The CDS on Lehman were settled smoothly, as was largely the case for the other 11 credit events occurring in 2008 that triggered payouts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colin_Barr_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_Barr-64">[64]</a></sup> And while it is arguable that other incidents would have been as bad or worse if less efficient instruments than CDS had been used for speculation and insurance purposes, the closing months of 2008 saw regulators working hard to reduce the risk involved in CDS transactions.</p>
<p>In 2008 there was no centralized <a href="/wiki/Exchange_(organized_market)" title="Exchange (organized market)">exchange</a> or <a href="/wiki/Clearing_house_(finance)" title="Clearing house (finance)">clearing house</a> for CDS transactions; they were all done <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_(finance)" title="Over-the-counter (finance)">over the counter</a> (OTC). This led to recent calls for the market to open up in terms of transparency and regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2008 the <a href="/wiki/Depository_Trust_%26_Clearing_Corporation" title="Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation">Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation</a> (DTCC), which runs a warehouse for CDS trade confirmations accounting for around 90% of the total market,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> announced that it will release market data on the outstanding notional of CDS trades on a weekly basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The data can be accessed on the DTCC's website here:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2010, Intercontinental Exchange, through its subsidiaries, ICE Trust in New York, launched in 2008, and ICE Clear Europe Limited in London, UK, launched in July 2009, clearing entities for credit default swaps (CDS) had cleared more than $10 trillion in credit default swaps (CDS) (Terhune Bloomberg Business Week 2010-07-29).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek1-71">[71]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[notes 1]</a></sup> Bloomberg's Terhune (2010) explained how investors seeking high-margin returns use Credit Default Swaps (CDS) to bet against financial instruments owned by other companies and countries. Intercontinental's clearing houses guarantee every transaction between buyer and seller providing a much-needed safety net reducing the impact of a default by spreading the risk. ICE collects on every trade.(Terhune Bloomberg Business Week 2010-07-29).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek1_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek1-71">[71]</a></sup> Brookings senior research fellow, Robert E. Litan, cautioned however, "valuable pricing data will not be fully reported, leaving ICE's institutional partners with a huge informational advantage over other traders. He calls ICE Trust "a derivatives dealers' club" in which members make money at the expense of nonmembers (Terhune citing Litan in Bloomberg Business Week 2010-07-29).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek1_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek1-71">[71]</a></sup> (Litan Derivatives Dealers’ Club 2010)." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litan_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Litan-73">[72]</a></sup> Actually, Litan conceded that "some limited progress toward central clearing of CDS has been made in recent months, with CDS contracts between dealers now being cleared centrally primarily through one clearinghouse (ICE Trust) in which the dealers have a significant financial interest (Litan 2010:6)." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litan_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Litan-73">[72]</a></sup> However, "as long as ICE Trust has a monopoly in clearing, watch for the dealers to limit the expansion of the products that are centrally cleared, and to create barriers to electronic trading and smaller dealers making competitive markets in cleared products (Litan 2010:8)." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litan_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Litan-73">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009 the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission granted an exemption for <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Exchange" title="Intercontinental Exchange">Intercontinental Exchange</a> to begin guaranteeing credit-default swaps. The SEC exemption represented the last regulatory approval needed by Atlanta-based Intercontinental.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg2009_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg2009-74">[73]</a></sup> A derivatives analyst at Morgan Stanley, one of the backers for IntercontinentalExchange's subsidiary, ICE Trust in New York, launched in 2008, claimed that the "clearinghouse, and changes to the contracts to standardize them, will probably boost activity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg2009_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg2009-74">[73]</a></sup> IntercontinentalExchange's subsidiary, ICE Trust's larger competitor, <a href="/wiki/CME_Group" title="CME Group">CME Group Inc.</a>, hasn’t received an SEC exemption, and agency spokesman John Nester said he didn’t know when a decision would be made.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_as_of_2009">Market as of 2009</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Market as of 2009">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The early months of 2009 saw several fundamental changes to the way CDSs operate, resulting from concerns over the instruments' safety after the events of the previous year. According to <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> managing director Athanassios Diplas "the industry pushed through 10 years worth of changes in just a few months". By late 2008 processes had been introduced allowing CDSs that offset each other to be cancelled. Along with termination of contracts that have recently paid out such as those based on Lehmans, this had by March reduced the face value of the market down to an estimated $30 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aline_Van_Duyn_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aline_Van_Duyn-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Bank for International Settlements estimates that outstanding derivatives total $708 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> U.S. and European regulators are developing separate plans to stabilize the derivatives market. Additionally there are some globally agreed standards falling into place in March 2009, administered by International Swaps and Derivatives Association (ISDA). Two of the key changes are:</p>
<p>1. The introduction of central clearing houses, one for the US and one for Europe. A clearing house acts as the central counterparty to both sides of a CDS transaction, thereby reducing the counterparty risk that both buyer and seller face.</p>
<p>2. The international standardization of CDS contracts, to prevent legal disputes in ambiguous cases where what the payout should be is unclear.</p>
<p>Speaking before the changes went live, Sivan Mahadevan, a derivatives analyst at Morgan Stanley,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg2009_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg2009-74">[73]</a></sup> one of the backers for IntercontinentalExchange's subsidiary, ICE Trust in New York, launched in 2008, claimed that</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">A clearinghouse, and changes to the contracts to standardize them, will probably boost activity. ... Trading will be much easier.... We'll see new players come to the market because they’ll like the idea of this being a better and more traded product. We also feel like over time we'll see the creation of different types of products (Mahadevan cited in Bloomberg 2009).</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the U.S., central clearing operations began in March 2009, operated by InterContinental Exchange (ICE). A key competitor also interested in entering the CDS clearing sector is CME Group.</p>
<p>In Europe, CDS Index clearing was launched by IntercontinentalExchange's European subsidiary ICE Clear Europe on July 31, 2009. It launched Single Name clearing in Dec 2009. By the end of 2009, it had cleared CDS contracts worth EUR 885 billion reducing the open interest down to EUR 75 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Report_Center_-_Data_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Report_Center_-_Data-77">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of 2009, banks had reclaimed much of their market share; hedge funds had largely retreated from the market after the crises. According to an estimate by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banque_de_France" title="Banque de France">Banque de France</a>, by late 2009 the bank JP Morgan alone now had about 30% of the global CDS market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoolsGold_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-FoolsGold-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Report_Center_-_Data_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Report_Center_-_Data-77">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_approvals_relating_to_ICE_and_its_competitor_CME">Government approvals relating to ICE and its competitor CME</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Government approvals relating to ICE and its competitor CME">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The SEC's approval for ICE Futures' request to be exempted from rules that would prevent it clearing CDSs was the third government action granted to Intercontinental in one week. On March 3, its proposed acquisition of Clearing Corp., a Chicago clearinghouse owned by eight of the largest dealers in the credit-default swap market, was approved by the Federal Trade Commission and the Justice Department. On March 5, 2009, the Federal Reserve Board, which oversees the clearinghouse, granted a request for ICE to begin clearing.</p>
<p>Clearing Corp. shareholders including JPMorgan Chase &amp; Co., Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and UBS AG, received $39 million in cash from Intercontinental in the acquisition, as well as the Clearing Corp.’s cash on hand and a 50-50 profit-sharing agreement with Intercontinental on the revenue generated from processing the swaps.</p>
<p>SEC spokesperson John Nestor stated</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">For several months the SEC and our fellow regulators have worked closely with all of the firms wishing to establish central counterparties.... We believe that CME should be in a position soon to provide us with the information necessary to allow the commission to take action on its exemptive requests.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Other proposals to clear credit-default swaps have been made by NYSE Euronext, Eurex AG and LCH.Clearnet Ltd. Only the NYSE effort is available now for clearing after starting on Dec. 22. As of Jan. 30, no swaps had been cleared by the NYSE’s London- based derivatives exchange, according to NYSE Chief Executive Officer Duncan Niederauer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
