<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clearing_house_member_requirements">Clearing house member requirements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Clearing house member requirements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Members of the Intercontinental clearinghouse ICE Trust (now ICE Clear Credit) in March 2009 would have to have a net worth of at least $5 billion and a credit rating of A or better to clear their credit-default swap trades. Intercontinental said in the statement today that all market participants such as hedge funds, banks or other institutions are open to become members of the clearinghouse as long as they meet these requirements.</p>
<p>A clearinghouse acts as the buyer to every seller and seller to every buyer, reducing the risk of counterparty defaulting on a transaction. In the over-the-counter market, where credit- default swaps are currently traded, participants are exposed to each other in case of a default. A clearinghouse also provides one location for regulators to view traders’ positions and prices.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="J.P._Morgan_losses">J.P. Morgan losses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: J.P. Morgan losses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2012, hedge fund insiders became aware that the market in credit default swaps was possibly being affected by the activities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bruno_Iksil" title="Bruno Iksil">Bruno Iksil</a>, a trader for J.P. Morgan Chase &amp; Co., referred to as "the London whale" in reference to the huge positions he was taking. Heavy opposing bets to his positions are known to have been made by traders, including another branch of J.P. Morgan, who purchased the derivatives offered by J.P. Morgan in such high volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTLR_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTLR-80">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2012_JPMorgan_Chase_trading_loss" title="2012 JPMorgan Chase trading loss">Major losses</a>, $2 billion, were reported by the firm in May 2012 in relationship to these trades. The disclosure, which resulted in headlines in the media, did not disclose the exact nature of the trading involved, which remains in progress. The item traded, possibly related to CDX IG 9, an index based on the default risk of major U.S. corporations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJwaves_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJwaves-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eFN_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-eFN-82">[81]</a></sup> has been described as a "derivative of a derivative".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNBlack_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNBlack-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DealBook2B_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-DealBook2B-84">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terms_of_a_typical_CDS_contract">Terms of a typical CDS contract</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Terms of a typical CDS contract">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A CDS contract is typically documented under a <i>confirmation</i> referencing the credit derivatives definitions as published by the <a href="/wiki/International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association" title="International Swaps and Derivatives Association">International Swaps and Derivatives Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> The confirmation typically specifies a <i>reference entity</i>, a corporation or sovereign that generally, although not always, has debt outstanding, and a <i>reference obligation</i>, usually an unsubordinated <a href="/wiki/Corporate_bond" title="Corporate bond">corporate bond</a> or <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">government bond</a>. The period over which default protection extends is defined by the contract <i>effective date</i> and <i>scheduled termination date</i>.</p>
<p>The confirmation also specifies a <i>calculation agent</i> who is responsible for making determinations as to <i>successors</i> and <i>substitute reference obligations</i> (for example necessary if the original reference obligation was a loan that is repaid before the expiry of the contract), and for performing various calculation and administrative functions in connection with the transaction. By market convention, in contracts between CDS dealers and end-users, the dealer is generally the calculation agent, and in contracts between CDS dealers, the protection seller is generally the calculation agent.</p>
<p>It is not the responsibility of the calculation agent to determine whether or not a credit event has occurred but rather a matter of fact that, pursuant to the terms of typical contracts, must be supported by <i>publicly available information</i> delivered along with a <i>credit event notice</i>. Typical CDS contracts do not provide an internal mechanism for challenging the occurrence or non-occurrence of a credit event and rather leave the matter to the courts if necessary, though actual instances of specific events being disputed are relatively rare.</p>
<p>CDS confirmations also specify the <i>credit events</i> that will give rise to payment obligations by the protection seller and delivery obligations by the protection buyer. Typical credit events include <i>bankruptcy</i> with respect to the reference entity and <i>failure to pay</i> with respect to its direct or guaranteed bond or loan debt. CDS written on North American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investment_grade" title="Investment grade">investment grade</a> corporate reference entities, European corporate reference entities and sovereigns generally also include <i>restructuring</i> as a credit event, whereas trades referencing North American <a href="/wiki/High-yield_debt" title="High-yield debt">high-yield</a> corporate reference entities typically do not.</p>
<p>Finally, standard CDS contracts specify <i>deliverable obligation characteristics</i> that limit the range of obligations that a protection buyer may deliver upon a credit event. Trading conventions for deliverable obligation characteristics vary for different markets and CDS contract types. Typical limitations include that deliverable debt be a bond or loan, that it have a maximum maturity of 30 years, that it not be subordinated, that it not be subject to transfer restrictions (other than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rule_144A" title="Rule 144A">Rule 144A</a>), that it be of a standard currency and that it not be subject to some contingency before becoming due.</p>
<p>The premium payments are generally quarterly, with maturity dates (and likewise premium payment dates) falling on March 20, June 20, September 20, and December 20. Due to the proximity to the <a href="/wiki/IMM_dates" title="IMM dates">IMM dates</a>, which fall on the third Wednesday of these months, these CDS maturity dates are also referred to as "IMM dates".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Credit_default_swap_and_sovereign_debt_crisis">Credit default swap and sovereign debt crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Credit default swap and sovereign debt crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_European_sovereign-debt_crisis" title="Causes of the European sovereign-debt crisis">Causes of the European sovereign-debt crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sovereign_credit_default_swaps.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="392" height="474" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sovereign_credit_default_swaps.png/300px-Sovereign_credit_default_swaps.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Sovereign_credit_default_swaps.png 1.5x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sovereign_credit_default_swaps.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sovereign credit default swap prices of selected European countries (2010-2011). The left axis is basis points, or 100ths of a percent; a level of 1,000 means it costs $1 million per year to protect $10 million of debt for five years.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The European sovereign debt crisis resulted from a combination of complex factors, including the <a href="/wiki/Financialization" title="Financialization">globalisation of finance</a>; easy credit conditions during the 2002–2008 period that encouraged high-risk lending and borrowing practices; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932012_global_financial_crisis" title="2007–2012 global financial crisis">2007–2012 global financial crisis</a>; international trade imbalances; <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_bubble" title="Real estate bubble">real-estate bubbles</a> that have since burst; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932012_global_recession" title="2008–2012 global recession">2008–2012 global recession</a>; fiscal policy choices related to government revenues and expenses; and approaches used by nations to bail out troubled banking industries and private bondholders, assuming private debt burdens or socialising losses. The Credit default swap market also reveals the beginning of the sovereign crisis.</p>
<p>Since December 1, 2011 the European Parliament has banned naked Credit default swap (CDS) on the debt for sovereign nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>The definition of restructuring is quite technical but is essentially intended to respond to circumstances where a reference entity, as a result of the deterioration of its credit, negotiates changes in the terms in its debt with its creditors as an alternative to formal insolvency proceedings (i.e. the debt is <i>restructured</i>). During the 2012 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_sovereign_debt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Greek sovereign debt (page does not exist)">Greek sovereign debt</a> crisis, one important issue was whether the restructuring would trigger Credit default swap (CDS) payments. European Central Bank and the International Monetary Fund negotiators avoided these triggers as they could have jeopardized the stability of major European banks who had been protection writers. An alternative could have been to create new CDS which clearly would pay in the event of debt restructuring. The market would have paid the spread between these and old (potentially more ambiguous) CDS. This practice is far more typical in jurisdictions that do not provide protective status to insolvent debtors similar to that provided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chapter_11" title="Chapter 11">Chapter 11</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Bankruptcy_Code" title="United States Bankruptcy Code">United States Bankruptcy Code</a>. In particular, concerns arising out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conseco" title="Conseco">Conseco</a>'s restructuring in 2000 led to the credit event's removal from North American high yield trades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Settlement">Settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_or_cash">Physical or cash</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Physical or cash">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As described in an earlier section, if a credit event occurs then CDS contracts can either be <i>physically settled</i> or <i>cash settled</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Bank_Report_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Bank_Report-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Physical settlement: The protection seller pays the buyer par value, and in return takes delivery of a debt obligation of the reference entity. For example, a hedge fund has bought $5 million worth of protection from a bank on the senior debt of a company. In the event of a default, the bank pays the hedge fund $5 million cash, and the hedge fund must deliver $5 million face value of senior debt of the company (typically bonds or loans, which are typically worth very little given that the company is in default).</li>
<li>Cash settlement: The protection seller pays the buyer the difference between par value and the market price of a debt obligation of the reference entity. For example, a hedge fund has bought $5 million worth of protection from a bank on the senior debt of a company. This company has now defaulted, and its senior bonds are now trading at 25 (i.e., 25 cents on the dollar) since the market believes that senior bondholders will receive 25% of the money they are owed once the company is wound up. Therefore, the bank must pay the hedge fund $5 million * (100%-25%) = $3.75 million.</li>
</ul>
<p>The development and growth of the CDS market has meant that on many companies there is now a much larger outstanding notional of CDS contracts than the outstanding notional value of its debt obligations. (This is because many parties made CDS contracts for speculative purposes, without actually owning any debt that they wanted to insure against default.) For example, at the time it filed for bankruptcy on September 14, 2008, Lehman Brothers had approximately $155 billion of outstanding debt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> but around $400 billion notional value of CDS contracts had been written that referenced this debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Clearly not all of these contracts could be physically settled, since there was not enough outstanding Lehman Brothers debt to fulfill all of the contracts, demonstrating the necessity for cash settled CDS trades. The trade confirmation produced when a CDS is traded states whether the contract is to be physically or cash settled.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Auctions">Auctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Auctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When a credit event occurs on a major company on which a lot of CDS contracts are written, an auction (also known as a <i>credit-fixing event</i>) may be held to facilitate settlement of a large number of contracts at once, at a fixed cash settlement price. During the auction process participating dealers (e.g., the big <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investment_banks" title="Investment banks">investment banks</a>) submit prices at which they would buy and sell the reference entity's debt obligations, as well as net requests for physical settlement against par. A second stage <a href="/wiki/Dutch_auction" title="Dutch auction">Dutch auction</a> is held following the publication of the initial midpoint of the dealer markets and what is the net open interest to deliver or be delivered actual bonds or loans. The final clearing point of this auction sets the final price for cash settlement of all CDS contracts and all physical settlement requests as well as matched limit offers resulting from the auction are actually settled. According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Swaps_and_Derivatives_Association" title="International Swaps and Derivatives Association">International Swaps and Derivatives Association</a> (ISDA), who organised them, auctions have recently proved an effective way of settling the very large volume of outstanding CDS contracts written on companies such as <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_Mutual" title="Washington Mutual">Washington Mutual</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isda.org_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-isda.org-90">[89]</a></sup> Commentator <a href="/wiki/Felix_Salmon" title="Felix Salmon">Felix Salmon</a>, however, has questioned in advance ISDA's ability to structure an auction, as defined to date, to set compensation associated with a 2012 bond swap in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_government_debt_crisis#Fifth_austerity_package_.E2.80.93_February_2012" title="Greek government debt crisis">Greek government debt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> For its part, ISDA in the leadup to a 50% or greater <a href="/wiki/Haircut_(finance)" title="Haircut (finance)">"haircut"</a> for Greek bondholders, issued an opinion that the bond swap would not constitute a default event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pricing_and_valuation">Pricing and valuation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pricing and valuation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are two competing theories usually advanced for the pricing of credit default swaps. The first, referred to herein as the 'probability model', takes the present value of a series of cashflows weighted by their probability of non-default. This method suggests that credit default swaps should trade at a considerably lower spread than corporate bonds.</p>
<p>The second model, proposed by <a href="/wiki/Darrell_Duffie" title="Darrell Duffie">Darrell Duffie</a>, but also by <a href="/wiki/John_C._Hull" title="John C. Hull">John Hull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_White_(economist)" title="Alan White (economist)">Alan White</a>, uses a no-arbitrage approach.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Probability_model">Probability model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Probability model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under the probability model, a credit default swap is priced using a model that takes four inputs; this is similar to the <a href="/wiki/RNPV" title="RNPV">rNPV</a> (risk-adjusted NPV) model used in <a href="/wiki/Drug_development" title="Drug development">drug development</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li>the "issue premium",</li>
<li>the recovery rate (percentage of notional repaid in event of default),</li>
<li>the "credit curve" for the reference entity and</li>
<li>the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LIBOR" title="LIBOR">LIBOR</a> curve".</li>
</ul>
<p>If <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">default</a> events never occurred the price of a CDS would simply be the sum of the <a href="/wiki/Discounted_cash_flow" title="Discounted cash flow">discounted</a> premium payments. So CDS pricing models have to take into account the possibility of a default occurring some time between the effective date and maturity date of the CDS contract. For the purpose of explanation we can imagine the case of a one-year CDS with effective date <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t_{0}}</annotation>
</semantics>

If the nominal for the CDS is <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>

<mi>c</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle c}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.017ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> then the size of the quarterly premium payments is <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>N</mi>
<mi>c</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>4</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Nc/4}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Nc/4" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/888c9768de1bc2f5855721f4aaca3e9cfc9cb832" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.437ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. If we assume for simplicity that defaults can only occur on one of the payment dates then there are five ways the contract could end:</p>
<ul>
<li>either it does not have any default at all, so the four premium payments are made and the contract survives until the maturity date, or</li>
<li>a default occurs on the first, second, third or fourth payment date.</li>
</ul>
<p>To price the CDS we now need to assign probabilities to the five possible outcomes, then calculate the present value of the payoff for each outcome. The present value of the CDS is then simply the <a href="/wiki/Present_value" title="Present value">present value</a> of the five payoffs multiplied by their probability of occurring.</p>
<p>This is illustrated in the following tree diagram where at each payment date either the contract has a default event, in which case it ends with a payment of <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mstyle>

<semantics>

At either side of the diagram are the cashflows up to that point in time with premium payments in blue and default payments in red. If the contract is terminated the square is shown with solid shading.</p>

The probability of no default occurring over a time period from <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.85ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> to <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>t</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t+\Delta t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t+\Delta t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e896baa706e07d8100439dc56c5b27771e354e4a" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:6.497ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">decays exponentially</a> with a time-constant determined by the credit spread, or mathematically <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>exp</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p=\exp(-s(t)\Delta t/(1-R))}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="p=\exp(-s(t)\Delta t/(1-R))" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4e375c43d881d25b3e71e3ebad85e97fb8b4d04" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-left: -0.079ex; width:26.979ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> where <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s(t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="s(t)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c484de351ba40ccb9a5ad522c29c1aac5686c0df" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.781ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Credit_spread_(bond)" title="Credit spread (bond)">credit spread</a> zero curve at time <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.85ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>. The riskier the reference entity the greater the spread and the more rapidly the survival probability decays with time.</p>
<p>To get the total present value of the credit default swap we multiply the probability of each outcome by its present value to give</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="No-arbitrage_model">No-arbitrage model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: No-arbitrage model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 'no-arbitrage' model proposed by both Duffie, and Hull-White, it is assumed that there is no risk free arbitrage. Duffie uses the LIBOR as the risk free rate, whereas Hull and White use US Treasuries as the risk free rate. Both analyses make simplifying assumptions (such as the assumption that there is zero cost of unwinding the fixed leg of the swap on default), which may invalidate the no-arbitrage assumption. However the Duffie approach is frequently used by the market to determine theoretical prices.</p>
<p>Under the Duffie construct, the price of a credit default swap can also be derived by calculating the asset swap spread of a bond. If a bond has a spread of 100, and the swap spread is 70 basis points, then a CDS contract should trade at 30. However, there are sometimes technical reasons why this will not be the case, and this may or may not present an arbitrage opportunity for the canny investor. The difference between the theoretical model and the actual price of a credit default swap is known as the basis.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticisms">Criticisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Criticisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Critics of the huge credit default swap market have claimed that it has been allowed to become too large without proper regulation and that, because all contracts are privately negotiated, the market has no transparency. Furthermore, there have been claims that CDSs exacerbated the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">2008 global financial crisis</a> by hastening the demise of companies such as Lehman Brothers and <a href="/wiki/American_International_Group" title="American International Group">AIG</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philips_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Philips-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the case of Lehman Brothers, it is claimed that the widening of the bank's CDS spread reduced confidence in the bank and ultimately gave it further problems that it was not able to overcome. However, proponents of the CDS market argue that this confuses cause and effect; CDS spreads simply reflected the reality that the company was in serious trouble. Furthermore, they claim that the CDS market allowed investors who had counterparty risk with Lehman Brothers to reduce their exposure in the case of their default.</p>
<p>Credit default swaps have also faced criticism that they contributed to a breakdown in negotiations during the 2009 <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Chapter_11_reorganization" title="General Motors Chapter 11 reorganization">General Motors Chapter 11 reorganization</a>, because certain bondholders might benefit from the credit event of a GM bankruptcy due to their holding of CDSs. Critics speculate that these creditors had an incentive to push for the company to enter bankruptcy protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> Due to a lack of transparency, there was no way to identify the protection buyers and protection writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was also feared at the time of Lehman's bankruptcy that the $400 billion notional of CDS protection which had been written on the bank could lead to a net payout of $366 billion from protection sellers to buyers (given the cash-settlement <a href="#Auctions">auction</a> settled at a final price of 8.625%) and that these large payouts could lead to further bankruptcies of firms without enough cash to settle their contracts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft.com_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft.com-97">[96]</a></sup> However, industry estimates after the auction suggest that net cashflows were only in the region of $7 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft.com_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-ft.com-97">[96]</a></sup> because many parties held offsetting positions. Furthermore, CDS deals are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark-to-market" title="Mark-to-market">marked-to-market</a> frequently. This would have led to margin calls from buyers to sellers as Lehman's CDS spread widened, reducing the net cashflows on the days after the auction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isda.org_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-isda.org-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Senior bankers have argued that not only has the CDS market functioned remarkably well during the financial crisis; that CDS contracts have been acting to distribute risk just as was intended; and that it is not CDSs themselves that need further regulation but the parties who trade them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some general criticism of financial derivatives is also relevant to credit derivatives. <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a> famously described derivatives bought speculatively as "financial weapons of mass destruction." In <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a>'s annual report to shareholders in 2002, he said, "Unless derivatives contracts are collateralized or guaranteed, their ultimate value also depends on the creditworthiness of the counterparties to them. In the meantime, though, before a contract is settled, the counterparties record profits and losses—often huge in amount—in their current earnings statements without so much as a penny changing hands. The range of derivatives contracts is limited only by the imagination of man (or sometimes, so it seems, madmen)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>To hedge the counterparty risk of entering a CDS transaction, one practice is to buy CDS protection on one's counterparty. The positions are marked-to-market daily and collateral pass from buyer to seller or vice versa to protect both parties against counterparty default, but money does not always change hands due to the offset of gains and losses by those who had both bought and sold protection. <a href="/wiki/Depository_Trust_%26_Clearing_Corporation" title="Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation">Depository Trust &amp; Clearing Corporation</a>, the clearinghouse for the majority of trades in the US over-the-counter market, stated in October 2008 that once offsetting trades were considered, only an estimated $6 billion would change hands on October 21, during the settlement of the CDS contracts issued on Lehman Brothers' debt, which amounted to somewhere between $150 to $360 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Buffett's criticism on derivatives, in October 2008 Berkshire Hathaway revealed to regulators that it has entered into at least $4.85 billion in derivative transactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> Buffett stated in his 2008 letter to shareholders that Berkshire Hathaway has no counterparty risk in its derivative dealings because Berkshire require counterparties to make payments when contracts are initiated, so that Berkshire always holds the money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Berkshire Hathaway was a large owner of Moody's stock during the period that it was one of two primary rating agencies for subprime CDOs, a form of mortgage security derivative dependent on the use of credit default swaps.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoline" title="Monoline">monoline</a> insurance companies got involved with writing credit default swaps on mortgage-backed CDOs. Some media reports have claimed this was a contributing factor to the downfall of some of the monolines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> In 2009 one of the monolines, <a href="/wiki/MBIA" title="MBIA">MBIA</a>, sued <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a>, claiming that Merrill had misrepresented some of its CDOs to MBIA in order to persuade MBIA to write CDS protection for those CDOs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systemic_risk">Systemic risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Systemic risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_financial_crisis_of_2008" title="Global financial crisis of 2008">2008 financial crisis</a>, counterparties became subject to a risk of default, amplified with the involvement of Lehman Brothers and AIG in a very large number of CDS transactions. This is an example of <a href="/wiki/Systemic_risk" title="Systemic risk">systemic risk</a>, risk which threatens an entire market, and a number of commentators have argued that size and deregulation of the CDS market have increased this risk.</p>
<p>For example, imagine if a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Mutual_fund" title="Mutual fund">mutual fund</a> had bought some <a href="/wiki/Washington_Mutual" title="Washington Mutual">Washington Mutual</a> corporate bonds in 2005 and decided to hedge their exposure by buying CDS protection from Lehman Brothers. After Lehman's default, this protection was no longer active, and Washington Mutual's sudden default only days later would have led to a massive loss on the bonds, a loss that should have been insured by the CDS. There was also fear that Lehman Brothers and AIG's inability to pay out on CDS contracts would lead to the unraveling of complex interlinked chain of CDS transactions between financial institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> So far this does not appear to have happened, although some commentators<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2009)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> have noted that because the total CDS exposure of a bank is not public knowledge, the fear that one could face large losses or possibly even default themselves was a contributing factor to the massive decrease in lending liquidity during September/October 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chains of CDS transactions can arise from a practice known as "netting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> Here, company B may buy a CDS from company A with a certain annual <i>premium</i>, say 2%. If the condition of the reference company worsens, the risk premium rises, so company B can sell a CDS to company C with a premium of say, 5%, and pocket the 3% difference. However, if the reference company defaults, company B might not have the assets on hand to make good on the contract. It depends on its contract with company A to provide a large payout, which it then passes along to company C.</p>
<p>The problem lies if one of the companies in the chain fails, creating a "<a href="/wiki/Domino_effect" title="Domino effect">domino effect</a>" of losses. For example, if company A fails, company B will default on its CDS contract to company C, possibly resulting in bankruptcy, and company C will potentially experience a large loss due to the failure to receive compensation for the bad debt it held from the reference company. Even worse, because CDS contracts are private, company C will not know that its fate is tied to company A; it is only doing business with company B.</p>
<p>As described <a href="#Market">above</a>, the establishment of a central exchange or clearing house for CDS trades would help to solve the "domino effect" problem, since it would mean that all trades faced a central counterparty guaranteed by a consortium of dealers.</p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Systemic_risk" title="Category:Systemic risk">Category:Systemic risk</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tax_and_accounting_issues">Tax and accounting issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Tax and accounting issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The U.S federal income tax treatment of CDS is uncertain (Nirenberg and Kopp 1997:1, Peaslee &amp; Nirenberg 2008-07-21:129 and Brandes 2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JournalTaxation1_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-JournalTaxation1-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peaslee_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peaslee-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brandes_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brandes-113">[112]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[notes 2]</a></sup> Commentators have suggested that, depending on how they are drafted, they are either <a href="/wiki/Notional_principal_contract" title="Notional principal contract">notional principal contracts</a> or <a href="/wiki/Option_(finance)" title="Option (finance)">options</a> for tax purposes,(Peaslee &amp; Nirenberg 2008-07-21:129).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peaslee_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peaslee-112">[111]</a></sup> but this is not certain. There is a risk of having CDS recharacterized as different types of financial instruments because they resemble put options and credit guarantees. In particular, the degree of risk depends on the type of settlement (physical/cash and binary/FMV) and trigger (default only/any credit event) (Nirenberg &amp; Kopp 1997:8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JournalTaxation1_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-JournalTaxation1-111">[110]</a></sup> And, as noted below, the appropriate treatment for Naked CDS may be entirely different.</p>
<p>If a CDS is a notional principal contract, pre-default periodic and nonperiodic payments on the swap are deductible and included in ordinary income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-115">[113]</a></sup> If a payment is a termination payment, or a payment received on a sale of the swap to a third party, however, its tax treatment is an open question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-115">[113]</a></sup> In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a> announced that it was studying the characterization of CDS in response to taxpayer confusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> As the outcome of its study, the IRS issued proposed regulations in 2011 specifically classifying CDS as notional principal contracts, and thereby qualifying such termination and sale payments for favorable capital gains tax treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup> These proposed regulations—which are yet to be finalized—have already been subject to criticism at a public hearing held by the IRS in January 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> as well as in the academic press,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Blakey_2013_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-James_Blakey_2013-119">[117]</a></sup> insofar as that classification would apply to Naked CDS.</p>
<p>The thrust of this criticism is that Naked CDS are indistinguishable from gambling wagers, and thus give rise in all instances to ordinary income, including to hedge fund managers on their so-called carried interests,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Blakey_2013_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-James_Blakey_2013-119">[117]</a></sup> and that the IRS exceeded its authority with the proposed regulations. This is evidenced by the fact that Congress confirmed that certain derivatives, including CDS, do constitute gambling when, in 2000, to allay industry fears that they were illegal gambling,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> it exempted them from “any State or local law that prohibits or regulates gaming.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> While this decriminalized Naked CDS, it did not grant them relief under the federal gambling tax provisions.</p>
<p>The accounting treatment of CDS used for hedging may not parallel the economic effects and instead, increase volatility. For example, GAAP generally require that CDS be reported on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_to_market" title="Mark to market">mark to market</a> basis. In contrast, assets that are held for investment, such as a commercial loan or bonds, are reported at cost, unless a probable and significant loss is expected. Thus, hedging a commercial loan using a CDS can induce considerable volatility into the <a href="/wiki/Income_statement" title="Income statement">income statement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balance_sheet" title="Balance sheet">balance sheet</a> as the CDS changes value over its life due to market conditions and due to the tendency for shorter dated CDS to sell at lower prices than longer dated CDS. One can try to account for the CDS as a hedge under FASB 133<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> but in practice that can prove very difficult unless the risky asset owned by the bank or corporation is exactly the same as the Reference Obligation used for the particular CDS that was bought.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="LCDS">LCDS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Credit_default_swap&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: LCDS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A new type of default swap is the "loan only" credit default swap (LCDS). This is conceptually very similar to a standard CDS, but unlike "vanilla" CDS, the underlying protection is sold on syndicated secured loans of the Reference Entity rather than the broader category of "Bond or Loan". Also, as of May 22, 2007, for the most widely traded LCDS form, which governs North American single name and index trades, the default settlement method for LCDS shifted to auction settlement rather than physical settlement. The auction method is essentially the same that has been used in the various ISDA cash settlement auction protocols, but does not require parties to take any additional steps following a credit event (i.e., adherence to a protocol) to elect cash settlement. On October 23, 2007, the first ever LCDS auction was held for <a href="/wiki/Movie_Gallery" title="Movie Gallery">Movie Gallery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because LCDS trades are linked to secured obligations with much higher recovery values than the unsecured bond obligations that are typically assumed the cheapest to deliver in respect of vanilla CDS, LCDS spreads are generally much tighter than CDS trades on the same name.</p>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Frielink, Karel (August 10, 2008). <a class="external text" href="http://www.curacao-law.com/2008/06/17/credit-default-swaps-and-insurance-issues-under-dutch-caribbean-law/" rel="nofollow">"Are credit default swaps insurance products?"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">November 3,</span> 2008</span>. <q>If the fund manager acts as the protection seller under a CDS, there is some risk of breach of insurance regulations for the manager.... There is no Netherlands Antilles case law or literature available which makes clear whether a CDS constitutes the ‘conducting of insurance business’ under Netherlands Antilles law. However, if certain requirements are met, credit derivatives do not qualify as an agreement of (non-life) insurance because such an arrangement would in those circumstances not contain all the elements necessary to qualify it as such.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACredit+default+swap&amp;rft.aufirst=Karel&amp;rft.aulast=Frielink&amp;rft.btitle=Are+credit+default+swaps+insurance+products%3F&amp;rft.date=2008-08-10&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.curacao-law.com%2F2008%2F06%2F17%2Fcredit-default-swaps-and-insurance-issues-under-dutch-caribbean-law%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li>October 19, 2008, Portfolio.com: "<a class="external text" href="http://www.portfolio.com/views/blogs/market-movers/2008/10/19/why-the-cds-market-didnt-fail" rel="nofollow">Why the CDS Market Didn't Fail</a>" Analyzes the CDS market's performance in the Lehman Bros. bankruptcy.</li>
<li>Boumlouka, Makrem (April 8, 2009), Wall Street Letter: "<a class="external text" href="http://www.wallstreetletter.com/Article.aspx?ArticleID=2177796" rel="nofollow">Credit Default Swap Market: "Big Bang"?</a> <sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link since December 2016">permanent dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup>".</li>
